software</a></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p><b>Microsoft PowerPoint</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Presentation_program" title="Presentation program">presentation program</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica-lead_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-lead-4">[4]</a></sup> created by Robert Gaskins and Dennis Austin<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica-lead_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-lead-4">[4]</a></sup> at a software company named Forethought, Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica-lead_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-lead-4">[4]</a></sup> It was released on April 20, 1987,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> initially for Macintosh computers only.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica-lead_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-lead-4">[4]</a></sup> Microsoft acquired PowerPoint for $14&#160;million three months after it appeared.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica-acquisition-lead_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-acquisition-lead-6">[6]</a></sup> This was Microsoft\'s first significant acquisition,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-lead_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-lead-7">[7]</a></sup> and Microsoft set up a new business unit for PowerPoint in Silicon Valley where Forethought had been located.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-lead_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-lead-7">[7]</a></sup></p>\n<p>PowerPoint became a component of the Microsoft Office suite, first offered in 1989 for Macintosh<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and in 1990 for Windows,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> which bundled several Microsoft apps. Beginning with PowerPoint 4.0 (1994), PowerPoint was integrated into Microsoft Office development, and adopted shared common components and a converged user interface.<sup id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001-lead_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-lead-10">[10]</a></sup></p>\n<p>PowerPoint\'s market share was very small at first, prior to introducing a version for Microsoft Windows, but grew rapidly with the growth of Windows and of Office.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(pp402\xe2\x80\x93404)</sup> Since the late 1990s, PowerPoint\'s worldwide market share of presentation software has been estimated at 95 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>\n<p>PowerPoint was originally designed to provide visuals for group presentations within business organizations, but has come to be very widely used in many other communication situations, both in business and beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica-uses-lead_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-uses-lead-13">[13]</a></sup> The impact of this much wider use of PowerPoint has been experienced as a powerful change throughout society,<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-14">[14]</a></sup> with strong reactions including advice that it should be used less,<sup id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">[15]</a></sup> should be used differently,<sup id="cite_ref-Mayer-Atkinson-2004_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayer-Atkinson-2004-16">[16]</a></sup> or should be used better.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The first PowerPoint version (Macintosh 1987) was used to produce overhead transparencies,<sup id="cite_ref-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead-18">[18]</a></sup> the second (Macintosh 1988, Windows 1990) could also produce color 35mm slides.<sup id="cite_ref-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead-18">[18]</a></sup> The third version (Windows and Macintosh 1992) introduced video output of virtual slideshows to digital projectors, which would over time completely replace physical transparencies and slides.<sup id="cite_ref-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead-18">[18]</a></sup> A dozen major versions since then have added many additional features and modes of operation<sup id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001-lead_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-lead-10">[10]</a></sup> and have made PowerPoint available beyond Apple Macintosh and Microsoft Windows, adding versions for iOS, Android, and web access.<sup id="cite_ref-Crowley_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crowley-19">[19]</a></sup></p>\n<p></p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc">\n<div class="toctitle">\n<h2>Contents</h2>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Use it better</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#U.S._military_excess"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">U.S. military excess</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Artistic_medium"><span class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">Artistic medium</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#PowerPoint_Viewer"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">PowerPoint Viewer</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Versions"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Versions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#File_formats"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">File formats</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Binary_.281987.E2.80.932007.29"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Binary (1987\xe2\x80\x932007)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Office_Open_XML_.28since_2007.29"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Office Open XML (since 2007)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Creation_at_Forethought_(1984\xe2\x80\x931987)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Creation_at_Forethought_.281984.E2.80.931987.29">Creation at Forethought (1984\xe2\x80\x931987)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Creation at Forethought (1984\xe2\x80\x931987)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>PowerPoint was created by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gaskins" title="Robert Gaskins">Robert Gaskins</a> and Dennis Austin at a software <a href="/wiki/Startup_company" title="Startup company">startup</a> in <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a> named <a href="/wiki/Forethought,_Inc." title="Forethought, Inc.">Forethought, Inc.</a><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Forethought had been founded in 1983 to create applications for future personal computers that would have graphical user interfaces, such as <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">Apple Macintosh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>\n<p>On July 5, 1984, Forethought hired Robert Gaskins as its vice president of product development, to create a new application that would be especially suited to the new <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">graphical</a> personal computers.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p51)</sup> Gaskins produced his initial description of PowerPoint about a month later (August 14, 1984) in the form of a 2-page document titled "Presentation Graphics for Overhead Projection."<sup id="cite_ref-presenter-proposal_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presenter-proposal-23">[23]</a></sup> By October 1984 Gaskins had selected Dennis Austin to be the developer for PowerPoint.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Gaskins and Austin worked together on the definition and design of the new product for nearly a year, and produced the first specification document dated August 21, 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This first design document showed a product as it would look in Microsoft <a href="/wiki/Windows_1.0" title="Windows 1.0">Windows 1.0</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> which at that time had not been released.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Development from that spec was begun by Austin in November 1985, for Macintosh first.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p104)</sup> About six months later, on May 1, 1986, Gaskins and Austin chose a second developer to join the project, Thomas Rudkin.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p149)</sup> Gaskins prepared two final product specification marketing documents in June 1986; these described a product for both Macintosh and Windows.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> At about the same time, Austin, Rudkin, and Gaskins produced a second and final major design specification document, this time showing a Macintosh look.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Throughout this development period the product was called "Presenter." Then, just before release, there was a last-minute check with Forethought\'s lawyers to register the name as a trademark, and "Presenter" was unexpectedly rejected because it had already been used by someone else. Gaskins says that he thought of "PowerPoint", based on the product\'s goal of "empowering" individual presenters, and sent that name to the lawyers for clearance, while all the documentation was hastily revised.<sup id="cite_ref-Indezine-2012_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indezine-2012-31">[31]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Funding to complete development of PowerPoint was assured in mid-January, 1987, when a new Apple Computer venture capital fund, called Apple\'s Strategic Investment Group,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> selected PowerPoint to be its first investment.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(pp169\xe2\x80\x93171)</sup> A month later, on February 22, 1987, Forethought announced PowerPoint at the Personal Computer Forum in Phoenix; <a href="/wiki/John_Sculley" title="John Sculley">John Sculley</a>, the CEO of Apple, appeared at the announcement and said "We see desktop presentation as potentially a bigger market for Apple than desktop publishing."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>\n<p>PowerPoint 1.0 for Macintosh shipped from manufacturing on April 20, 1987, and the first production run of 10,000 units was sold out.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span id="Acquisition_by_Microsoft_(1987\xe2\x80\x931992)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisition_by_Microsoft_.281987.E2.80.931992.29">Acquisition by Microsoft (1987\xe2\x80\x931992)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Acquisition by Microsoft (1987\xe2\x80\x931992)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>By early 1987, Microsoft was starting to plan a new application to create presentations, an activity led by <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Raikes" title="Jeff Raikes">Jeff Raikes</a>, who was head of marketing for the Applications Division.<sup id="cite_ref-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist-35">[35]</a></sup> Microsoft assigned an internal group to write a specification and plan for a new presentation product.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> They contemplated an acquisition to speed up development, and in early 1987 Microsoft sent a letter of intent to acquire <a href="/wiki/Dave_Winer" title="Dave Winer">Dave Winer</a>\'s product called <a href="/wiki/MORE_(application)" title="MORE (application)">MORE</a>, an outlining program that could print its outlines as bullet charts.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> During this preparatory activity Raikes discovered that a program specifically to make overhead presentations was already being developed by Forethought, Inc., and that it was nearly completed.<sup id="cite_ref-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist-35">[35]</a></sup> Raikes and others visited Forethought on February 6, 1987, for a confidential demonstration.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p173)</sup></p>\n<p>Raikes later recounted his reaction to seeing PowerPoint and his report about it to <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gates" title="Bill Gates">Bill Gates</a>, who was initially skeptical:<sup id="cite_ref-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>I thought, "software to do overheads\xe2\x80\x94that\'s a great idea." I came back to see Bill. I said, "Bill, I think we really ought to do this;" and Bill said, "No, no, no, no, no, that\'s just a feature of Microsoft Word, just put it into Word." ... And I kept saying, "Bill, no, it\'s not just a feature of Microsoft Word, it\'s a whole genre of how people do these presentations." And, to his credit, he listened to me and ultimately allowed me to go forward and ... buy this company in Silicon Valley called Forethought, for the product known as PowerPoint.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>When PowerPoint was released by Forethought, its initial press was favorable; the <i>Wall Street Journal</i> reported on early reactions: <span style="padding-right:.2em;">"</span>\'I see about one product a year I get this excited about,\' says Amy Wohl, a consultant in Bala Cynwyd, Pa. \'People will buy a Macintosh just to get access to this product.<span style="padding-right:.2em;">\'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>\n<p>On April 28, 1987, a week after shipment, a group of Microsoft\'s senior executives spent another day at Forethought to hear about initial PowerPoint sales on Macintosh and plans for Windows.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p191)</sup> The following day, Microsoft sent a letter to Dave Winer withdrawing its earlier letter of intent to acquire his company,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and in mid-May 1987 Microsoft sent a letter of intent to acquire Forethought.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> As requested in that letter of intent, Robert Gaskins from Forethought went to Redmond for a one-on-one meeting with Bill Gates in early June, 1987,<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p197)</sup> and by the end of July an agreement was concluded for an acquisition. The <i>New York Times</i> reported:<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>... July 30\xe2\x80\x94 The Microsoft Corporation announced its first significant software acquisition today, paying $14&#160;million [$29.5&#160;million in present-day terms<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-42">[42]</a></sup>] for Forethought Inc. of Sunnyvale, Calif. Forethought makes a program called PowerPoint that allows users of Apple Macintosh computers to make overhead transparencies or flip charts. ... [T]he acquisition of Forethought is the first significant one for Microsoft, which is based in Redmond, Wash. Forethought would remain in Sunnyvale, giving Microsoft a Silicon Valley presence. The unit will be headed by Robert Gaskins, Forethought\'s vice president of product development.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Microsoft\'s president <a href="/wiki/Jon_Shirley" title="Jon Shirley">Jon Shirley</a> offered Microsoft\'s motivation for the acquisition: <span style="padding-right:.2em;">"</span>\'We made this deal primarily because of our belief in desktop presentations as a product category. ... Forethought was first to market with a product in this category.<span style="padding-right:.2em;">\'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-Shirley-comments_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shirley-comments-43">[43]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Microsoft set up within its Applications Division an independent "Graphics Business Unit" to develop and to market PowerPoint, the first Microsoft application group distant from the main Redmond location.<sup id="cite_ref-Shirley-comments_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shirley-comments-43">[43]</a></sup> All the PowerPoint people from Forethought joined Microsoft, and the new location was headed by Robert Gaskins, with Dennis Austin and Thomas Rudkin leading development.<sup id="cite_ref-GBU-first-year-1988_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GBU-first-year-1988-44">[44]</a></sup> PowerPoint 1.0 for Macintosh was modified to indicate the new Microsoft ownership and continued to be sold.<sup id="cite_ref-GBU-first-year-1988_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GBU-first-year-1988-44">[44]</a></sup></p>\n<p>A new PowerPoint 2.0 for Macintosh, adding color 35mm slides, appeared by mid-1988,<sup id="cite_ref-GBU-first-year-1988_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GBU-first-year-1988-44">[44]</a></sup> and again won good reviews.<sup id="cite_ref-pournelle198901_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pournelle198901-45">[45]</a></sup> The same PowerPoint 2.0 product re-developed for Windows was shipped two years later, in mid-1990, at the same time as <a href="/wiki/Windows_3.0" title="Windows 3.0">Windows 3.0</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Much of the color technology was the fruit of a joint development partnership with <a href="/wiki/Genigraphics" title="Genigraphics">Genigraphics</a>, at that time the dominant presentation services company.<sup id="cite_ref-GBU-first-year-genigraphics_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GBU-first-year-genigraphics-47">[47]</a></sup></p>\n<p>PowerPoint 3.0, which was shipped in 1992 for both Windows and Mac, added live video for projectors and monitors, with the result that PowerPoint was thereafter used for delivering presentations as well as for preparing them. This was at first an alternative to overhead transparencies and 35mm slides, but over time would come to replace them.<sup id="cite_ref-CACM-2007-Gaskins-history_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CACM-2007-Gaskins-history-48">[48]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span id="Part_of_Microsoft_Office_(since_1993)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Part_of_Microsoft_Office_.28since_1993.29">Part of Microsoft Office (since 1993)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Part of Microsoft Office (since 1993)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Microsoft_Office" title="History of Microsoft Office">History of Microsoft Office</a></div>\n<p>PowerPoint had been included in <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office" title="Microsoft Office">Microsoft Office</a> from the beginning. PowerPoint 2.0 for Macintosh was part of the first Office bundle for Macintosh which was offered in mid-1989.<sup id="cite_ref-mac-office-1989_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mac-office-1989-49">[49]</a></sup> When PowerPoint 2.0 for Windows appeared, a year later, it was part of a similar Office bundle for Windows, which was offered in late 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-win-office-1990_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-win-office-1990-50">[50]</a></sup> Both of these were bundling promotions, in which the independent applications were packaged together and offered for a lower total price.<sup id="cite_ref-mac-office-1989_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mac-office-1989-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-win-office-1990_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-win-office-1990-50">[50]</a></sup></p>\n<p>PowerPoint 3.0 (1992) was again separately specified and developed,<sup id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001-hist_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-hist-51">[51]</a></sup> and was prominently advertised and sold separately from Office.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> It was, as before, included in <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_3.0" title="Microsoft Office 3.0">Microsoft Office 3.0</a>, both for Windows and the corresponding version for Macintosh.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>\n<p>A plan to integrate the applications themselves more tightly had been indicated as early as February 1991, toward the end of PowerPoint 3.0 development, in an internal memo by Bill Gates:<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>Another important question is what portion of our applications sales over time will be a set of applications versus a single product. ... Please assume that we stay ahead in integrating our family together in evaluating our future strategies\xe2\x80\x94the product teams WILL deliver on this. ... I believe that we should position the "OFFICE" as our most important application.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The move from bundling separate products to integrated development began with PowerPoint 4.0, developed in 1993\xe2\x80\x931994 under new management from Redmond.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The PowerPoint group in Silicon Valley was reorganized from the independent "Graphics Business Unit" (GBU) to become the "Graphics Product Unit" (GPU) for Office, and PowerPoint 4.0 changed to adopt a converged user interface and other components shared with the other apps in Office.<sup id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001-hist_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-hist-51">[51]</a></sup></p>\n<p>When it was released, the computer press reported on the change approvingly: "PowerPoint 4.0 has been re-engineered from the ground up to resemble and work with the latest applications in Office: Word 6.0, Excel 5.0, and Access 2.0. The integration is so good, you\'ll have to look twice to make sure you\'re running PowerPoint and not Word or Excel."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Office integration was further underscored in the following version, PowerPoint 95, which was given the version number PowerPoint 7.0 (skipping 5.0 and 6.0) so that all the components of Office would share the same major version number.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Although PowerPoint by this point had become part of the integrated Microsoft Office product, its development remained in Silicon Valley. Succeeding versions of PowerPoint introduced important changes, particularly version 12.0 (2007) which had a very different shared Office "<a href="/wiki/Ribbon_(computing)" title="Ribbon (computing)">ribbon</a>" user interface, and a new shared <a href="/wiki/Office_Open_XML" title="Office Open XML">Office XML-based file format</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> This marked the 20th anniversary of PowerPoint, and Microsoft held an event to commemorate that anniversary at its Silicon Valley Campus for the PowerPoint team there. Special guests were Robert Gaskins, Dennis Austin, and Thomas Rudkin, and the featured speaker was Jeff Raikes, all from PowerPoint 1.0 days, 20 years before.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Since then major development of PowerPoint as part of Office has continued. New development techniques<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> (shared across Office) for PowerPoint 2016 have made it possible to ship versions of PowerPoint 2016 for Windows, Mac, iOS, Android, and web access nearly simultaneously,<sup id="cite_ref-Crowley_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crowley-19">[19]</a></sup> and to release new features on a nearly-monthly schedule.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> PowerPoint development is still located in Silicon Valley as of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2010, Jeff Raikes, who had most recently been President of the Business Division of Microsoft (including responsibility for Office),<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> observed: "of course, today we know that PowerPoint is often times the number two\xe2\x80\x94or in some cases even the number one\xe2\x80\x94most-used tool" among the applications in Office.<sup id="cite_ref-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sales_and_market_share">Sales and market share</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sales and market share">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>PowerPoint\'s initial sales were about 40,000 copies sold in 1987 (nine months), about 85,000 copies in 1988, and about 100,000 copies in 1989, all for Macintosh.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-64">[64]</a></sup> PowerPoint\'s market share in its first three years was a tiny part of the total presentation market, which was very heavily dominated by <a href="/wiki/MS-DOS" title="MS-DOS">MS-DOS</a> applications on PCs.<sup id="cite_ref-PC-market-share-2005_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PC-market-share-2005-65">[65]</a></sup> The market leaders on MS-DOS in 1988-1989<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> were <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Graphics" title="Harvard Graphics">Harvard Graphics</a> (introduced by <a href="/wiki/Software_Publishing_Corporation" title="Software Publishing Corporation">Software Publishing</a> in 1986<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>) in first place, and <a href="/wiki/IBM_Lotus_Freelance_Graphics" title="IBM Lotus Freelance Graphics">Lotus Freelance Plus</a> (also introduced in 1986<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>) as a strong second.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> They were competing with more than a dozen other MS-DOS presentation products,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and Microsoft did not develop a PowerPoint version for MS-DOS.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> After three years, PowerPoint sales were disappointing. Jeff Raikes, who had bought PowerPoint for Microsoft, later recalled: "By 1990, it looked like it wasn\'t a very smart idea [for Microsoft to have acquired PowerPoint], because not very many people were using PowerPoint."<sup id="cite_ref-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<p>This began to change when the first version for Windows, PowerPoint 2.0, brought sales up to about 200,000 copies in 1990 and to about 375,000 copies in 1991, with Windows units outselling Macintosh.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p403)</sup> PowerPoint sold about 1&#160;million copies in 1992, of which about 80 percent were for Windows and about 20 percent for Macintosh,<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p403)</sup> and in 1992 PowerPoint\'s market share of worldwide presentation graphics software sales was reported as 63 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_64-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p404)</sup> By the last six months of 1992, PowerPoint revenue was running at a rate of over $100&#160;million annually ($211&#160;million in present-day terms<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-42">[42]</a></sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_64-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p405)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Sales of PowerPoint 3.0 doubled to about 2&#160;million copies in 1993, of which about 90 percent were for Windows and about 10 percent for Macintosh,<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_64-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p403)</sup> and in 1993 PowerPoint\'s market share of worldwide presentation graphics software sales was reported as 78 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_64-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p404)</sup> In both years, about half of total revenue came from sales outside the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_64-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p404)</sup></p>\n<p>By 1997 PowerPoint sales had doubled again, to more than 4&#160;million copies annually, representing 85 percent of the world market.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Also in 1997, an internal publication from the PowerPoint group said that by then over 20&#160;million copies of PowerPoint were in use, and that total revenues from PowerPoint over its first ten years (1987 to 1996) had already exceeded $1&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Since the late 1990s, PowerPoint\'s market share of total world presentation software has been estimated at 95 percent by both industry and academic sources.<sup id="cite_ref-Thielsch-Perabo-2012-history_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thielsch-Perabo-2012-history-75">[75]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operation">Operation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Operation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The earliest version of PowerPoint (1987 for Macintosh) could be used to print black and white pages to be photocopied onto <a href="/wiki/Transparency_(projection)" title="Transparency (projection)">sheets of transparent film</a> for projection from <a href="/wiki/Overhead_projector" title="Overhead projector">overhead projectors</a>, and to print speaker\'s notes and audience handouts; the next version (1988 for Macintosh, 1990 for Windows) was extended to also produce <a href="/wiki/Presentation_slide" title="Presentation slide">color 35mm slides</a> by communicating a file over a modem to a <a href="/wiki/Genigraphics" title="Genigraphics">Genigraphics</a> imaging center with slides returned by overnight delivery for projection from <a href="/wiki/Slide_projector" title="Slide projector">slide projectors</a>. PowerPoint was used for planning and preparing a presentation, but not for delivering it (apart from previewing it on a computer screen, or distributing printed paper copies).<sup id="cite_ref-CACM-2007-Gaskins-operation_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CACM-2007-Gaskins-operation-76">[76]</a></sup> The operation of PowerPoint changed substantially in its third version (1992 for Windows and Macintosh), when PowerPoint was extended to also deliver a presentation by producing direct video output to <a href="/wiki/Video_projector" title="Video projector">digital projectors</a> or large monitors.<sup id="cite_ref-CACM-2007-Gaskins-operation_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CACM-2007-Gaskins-operation-76">[76]</a></sup> In 1992 video projection of presentations was rare and expensive, and practically unknown from a laptop computer. Robert Gaskins, one of the creators of PowerPoint, says he publicly demonstrated that use for the first time at a large Microsoft meeting held in Paris on February 25, 1992, by using an unreleased development build of PowerPoint 3.0 running on an early pre-production sample of a powerful new color laptop and feeding a <a href="/wiki/Talaria_projector" title="Talaria projector">professional auditorium video projector</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-Gaskins-operation_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-Gaskins-operation-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(pp373\xe2\x80\x93375)</sup></p>\n<p>By about 2003, ten years later, digital projection had become the dominant mode of use, replacing transparencies and 35mm slides and their projectors.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-Gaskins-operation_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-Gaskins-operation-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(pp410\xe2\x80\x93414)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> As a result, the meaning of "PowerPoint presentation" narrowed to mean specifically digital projection:<sup id="cite_ref-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007-79">[79]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>... in the business lexicon, "PowerPoint presentation" had come to refer to a presentation made using a PowerPoint slideshow projected from a computer. Although the PowerPoint software had been used to generate transparencies for over a decade, this usage was not typically encompassed by common understanding of the term.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>In contemporary operation, PowerPoint is used to create a file (called a "presentation" or "deck"<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>) containing a sequence of pages (called "slides" in the app) which usually have a consistent style (from template masters), and which may contain information imported from other apps or created in PowerPoint, including text, bullet lists, tables, charts, drawn shapes, images, audio clips, video clips, animations of elements, and animated transitions between slides, plus attached notes for each slide.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>\n<p>After such a file is created, typical operation is to present it as a <a href="/wiki/Slide_show" title="Slide show">slide show</a> using a portable computer, where the presentation file is stored on the computer or available from a network, and the computer\'s screen shows a "presenter view" with current slide, next slide, speaker\'s notes for the current slide, and other information.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Video is sent from the computer to one or more external digital projectors or monitors, showing only the current slide to the audience, with sequencing controlled by the speaker at the computer. A smartphone remote control built in to PowerPoint for iOS (optionally controlled from Apple Watch)<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> and for Android<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> allows the presenter to control the show from elsewhere in the room.</p>\n<p>In addition to a computer slide show projected to a live audience by a speaker, PowerPoint can be used to deliver a presentation in a number of other ways:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Displayed on the screen of the presentation computer or tablet (for a very small group)<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Printed for distribution as paper documents (in several formats)<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Distributed as files for private viewing, even on computers without PowerPoint<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Packaged for distribution on CD or a network, including linked and embedded data<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Transmitted as a live broadcast presentation over the web<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Embeded in a web page or blog<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Shared on social networks such as <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> or <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Set up as a self-running unattended display<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Recorded as video/audio (<a href="/wiki/H.264" class="mw-redirect" title="H.264">H.264</a>/<a href="/wiki/Advanced_Audio_Coding" title="Advanced Audio Coding">AAC</a>), to be distributed as for any other video<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>\n</ul>\n<p>Some of these ways of using PowerPoint have been studied by <a href="/wiki/JoAnne_Yates" title="JoAnne Yates">JoAnne Yates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wanda_Orlikowski" title="Wanda Orlikowski">Wanda Orlikowski</a> of the <a href="/wiki/MIT_Sloan_School_of_Management" title="MIT Sloan School of Management">MIT Sloan School of Management</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007-79">[79]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>The standard form of such presentations involves a single person standing before a group of people, talking and using the PowerPoint slideshow to project visual aids onto a screen. ... In practice, however, presentations are not always delivered in this mode. In our studies, we often found that the presenter sat at a table with a small group of people and walked them through a "deck", composed of paper copies of the slides. In some cases, decks were simply distributed to individuals, without even a walk-through or discussion. ... Other variations in form included sending the PowerPoint file electronically to another site and talking through the slides over an audio or video channel (e.g., telephone or video conference) as both parties viewed the slides. ... Another common variation was placing a PowerPoint file on a web site for people to view at different times.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>They found that some of these ways of using PowerPoint could influence the content of presentations, for example when "the slides themselves have to carry more of the substance of the presentation, and thus need considerably more content than they would have if they were intended for projection by a speaker who would orally provide additional details and nuance about content and context."<sup id="cite_ref-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007-79">[79]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">Cultural impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cultural impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Presentation_in_Progress.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Presentation_in_Progress.jpg/220px-Presentation_in_Progress.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Presentation_in_Progress.jpg/330px-Presentation_in_Progress.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Presentation_in_Progress.jpg/440px-Presentation_in_Progress.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Presentation_in_Progress.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nA PowerPoint presentation in progress</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>PowerPoint, more than most other personal computer applications, has been experienced as a powerful force producing change throughout all of society. In 2016 an analyst summed up: "the real mystery is ... \'how come almost every organisation in the world is using PowerPoint to communicate almost everything to almost everybody?\'. That\'s the real question. How come PowerPoint is everywhere?"<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-14">[14]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_uses">Business uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Business uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>PowerPoint was originally targeted just for business presentations. Robert Gaskins, who was responsible for its design, has written about his intended customers: "... I did not target other existing large groups of users of presentations, such as school teachers or military officers. ... I also did not plan to target people who were not existing users of presentations ... such as clergy and school children ... . Our focus was purely on business users, in small and large companies, from one person to the largest multinationals."<sup id="cite_ref-Gaskins-Sweating-Bullets-2012-cultural_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaskins-Sweating-Bullets-2012-cultural-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(pp76\xe2\x80\x9377)</sup> Business people had for a long time made presentations for sales calls and for internal company communications, and PowerPoint produced the same formats in the same style and for the same purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaskins-Sweating-Bullets-2012-cultural_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaskins-Sweating-Bullets-2012-cultural-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p420)</sup></p>\n<p>PowerPoint use in business grew over its first five years (1987-1992) to sales of about 1 million copies annually, for worldwide market share of 63 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Over the following five years (1992-1997) PowerPoint sales accelerated, to a rate of about 4 million copies annually, for worldwide market share of 85 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The increase in business use has been attributed to "<a href="/wiki/Network_effect" title="Network effect">network effects</a>," whereby additional users of PowerPoint in a company or an industry increased its salience and value to other users.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Not everyone immediately approved of the greater use of PowerPoint for presentations, even in business. CEOs who very early were reported to discourage or ban PowerPoint presentations at internal business meetings included <a href="/wiki/Louis_V._Gerstner_Jr." title="Louis V. Gerstner Jr.">Lou Gerstner</a> (at IBM, in 1993),<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scott_McNealy" title="Scott McNealy">Scott McNealy</a> (at Sun Microsystems, in 1996),<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jobs" title="Steve Jobs">Steve Jobs</a> (at Apple, in 1997).<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> But even so, Rich Gold, a scholar who studied corporate presentation use at <a href="/wiki/PARC_(company)" title="PARC (company)">Xerox PARC</a>, could write in 1999: "Within today\'s corporation, if you want to communicate an idea ... you use PowerPoint."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_beyond_business">Uses beyond business</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Uses beyond business">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>At the same time that PowerPoint was becoming dominant in business settings, it was also being adopted for uses beyond business: "Personal computing ... scaled up the production of presentations. ... The result has been the rise of presentation culture. In an information society, nearly everyone presents."<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 1998, at about the same time that Gold was pronouncing PowerPoint\'s ubiquity in business, the influential <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Labs</a> engineer <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Lucky" title="Robert W. Lucky">Robert W. Lucky</a> could already write about broader uses:<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>... the world has run amok with the giddy power of presentation graphics. A new language is in the air, and it is codified in PowerPoint. ... In a family discussion about what to do on a given evening, for example, I feel like pulling out my laptop and giving a <a href="/wiki/Viewgraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Viewgraph">Vugraph</a> presentation ... . In church I am surprised that the preachers haven\'t caught on yet. ... How have we gotten on so long without PowerPoint?</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Over a decade or so, beginning in the mid 1990s, PowerPoint began to be used in many communication situations, well beyond its original business presentation uses, to include teaching in schools<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> and in universities,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> lecturing in scientific meetings<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> (and preparing their related poster sessions<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>), worshipping in churches,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> making legal arguments in courtrooms,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> displaying supertitles in theaters,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> driving helmet-mounted displays in spacesuits for NASA astronauts,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> giving military briefings,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> issuing governmental reports,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> undertaking diplomatic negotiations,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> writing novels,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> giving architectural demonstrations,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> prototyping website designs,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> creating animated video games,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> creating art projects,<sup id="cite_ref-Vienne_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vienne-120">[120]</a></sup> and even as a substitute for writing engineering technical reports,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> and as an organizing tool for writing general business documents.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>\n<p>By 2003, it seemed that PowerPoint was being used everywhere. Julia Keller reported for the <i>Chicago Tribune</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>PowerPoint ... is one of the most pervasive and ubiquitous technological tools ever concocted. In less than a decade, it has revolutionized the worlds of business, education, science and communications, swiftly becoming the standard for just about anybody who wants to explain just about anything to just about anybody else. From corporate middle managers reporting on production goals to 4th-graders fashioning a show-and-tell on the French and Indian War to church pastors explicating the seven deadly sins ... PowerPoint seems poised for world domination.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_reactions">Cultural reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cultural reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As uses broadened, cultural awareness of PowerPoint grew and commentary about it began to appear. "With the widespread adoption of PowerPoint came complaints ... often very general statements reflecting dissatisfaction with modern media and communication practices as well as the dysfunctions of organizational culture."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Indications of this awaremess included increasing mentions of PowerPoint use in the <a href="/wiki/Dilbert" title="Dilbert">Dilbert</a> comic strips of <a href="/wiki/Scott_Adams" title="Scott Adams">Scott Adams</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> comic parodies of poor or inappropriate use such as the <a href="/wiki/Gettysburg_Address" title="Gettysburg Address">Gettysburg Address</a> in PowerPoint<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> or summaries of Shakespeare\'s <i>Hamlet</i> and Nabokov\'s <i>Lolita</i> in PowerPoint,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> and a vast number of publications on the general subject of PowerPoint, especially about how to use it.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Out of all the analyses of PowerPoint over a quarter of a century, at least three general themes emerged as categories of reaction to its broader use: (1) "Use it less": avoid PowerPoint in favor of alternatives, such as using more-complex graphics and written prose, or using nothing;<sup id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">[15]</a></sup> (2) "Use it differently": make a major change to a PowerPoint style that is simpler and pictorial, turning the presentation toward a performance, more like a Steve Jobs keynote;<sup id="cite_ref-Mayer-Atkinson-2004_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayer-Atkinson-2004-16">[16]</a></sup> and (3) "Use it better": retain much of the conventional PowerPoint style but learn to avoid making many kinds of mistakes that can interfere with communication.<sup id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-2007_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-2007-130">[130]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_it_less">Use it less</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Use it less">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Edward_Tufte" title="Edward Tufte">Edward Tufte</a></div>\n<p>An early reaction was that the broader use of PowerPoint was a mistake, and should be reversed. An influential example of this came from <a href="/wiki/Edward_Tufte" title="Edward Tufte">Edward Tufte</a>, an authority on information design, who has been a professor of political science, statistics, and computer science at Princeton and Yale, but is best known for his self-published books on data visualization, which have sold nearly 2&#160;million copies as of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2003, he published a widely-read booklet titled <i>The Cognitive Style of PowerPoint,</i> revised in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">[15]</a></sup> Tufte found a number of problems with the "cognitive style" of PowerPoint, many of which he attributed to the standard default style templates:<sup id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">[15]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>PowerPoint\'s convenience for some presenters is costly to the content and the audience. These costs arise from the <i>cognitive style characteristics of the standard default PP presentation:</i> foreshortening of evidence and thought, low spatial resolution, an intensely hierarchical single-path structure as the model for organizing every type of content, breaking up narratives and data into slides and minimal fragments, rapid temporal sequencing of thin information rather than focused spatial analysis, conspicuous chartjunk and PP Phluff, branding of slides with logotypes, a preoccupation with format not content, incompetent designs for data graphics and tables, and a smirky commercialism that turns information into a sales pitch and presenters into marketeers <i>[italics in original]</i>.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Tufte particularly advised against using PowerPoint for reporting scientific analyses, using as a dramatic example some slides made during the flight of the space shuttle Columbia after it had been damaged by an accident at liftoff, slides which poorly communicated the engineers\' limited understanding of what had happened.<sup id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(pp8\xe2\x80\x9314)</sup> For such technical presentations, and for most occasions apart from its initial domain of sales presentations, Tufte advised against using PowerPoint at all; in many situations, according to Tufte, it would be better to substitute high-resolution graphics or concise prose documents as handouts for the audience to study and discuss, providing a great deal more detail.<sup id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">[15]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Many commentators enthusiastically joined in Tufte\'s vivid criticism of PowerPoint uses,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> and at a conference held in 2013 (a decade after Tufte\'s booklet appeared) one paper claimed that "Despite all the criticism about his work, Tufte can be considered as the single most influential author in the discourse on PowerPoint. ... While his approach was not rigorous from a research perspective, his articles received wide resonance with the public at large ... ." <sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> There were also others who disagreed with Tufte\'s assertion that the PowerPoint program reduces the quality of presenters\' thoughts: <a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">Steven Pinker</a>, professor of psychology at MIT and later Harvard, had earlier argued that "If anything, PowerPoint, if used well, would ideally reflect the way we think."<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Pinker later reinforced this opinion: "Any general opposition to PowerPoint is just dumb, ... It\'s like denouncing lectures\xe2\x80\x94before there were awful PowerPoint presentations, there were awful scripted lectures, unscripted lectures, slide shows, chalk talks, and so on."<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Much of the early commentary, on all sides, was "informal" and "anecdotal", because empirical research had been limited.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_it_differently">Use it differently</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Use it differently">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._Mayer" title="Richard E. Mayer">Richard E. Mayer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stevenote" title="Stevenote">Steve Jobs Keynotes</a></div>\n<p>A second reaction to PowerPoint use was to say that PowerPoint can be used well, but only by substantially changing its style of use. This reaction is exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._Mayer" title="Richard E. Mayer">Richard E. Mayer</a>, a professor of psychology at the University of California, Santa Barbara, who has studied cognition and learning, particularly the design of educational multimedia, and who has published more than 500 publications, including over 30 books.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Mayer\'s theme has been that "In light of the science, it is up to us to make a fundamental shift in our thinking\xe2\x80\x94we can no longer expect people to struggle to try to adapt to our PowerPoint habits. Instead, we have to change our PowerPoint habits to align with the way people learn."<sup id="cite_ref-Mayer-Atkinson-2004_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayer-Atkinson-2004-16">[16]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Tufte had argued his judgment that the information density of text on PowerPoint slides was too low, perhaps only 40 words on a slide, leading to over-simplified messages;<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Mayer responded that his empirical research showed exactly the opposite, that the amount of text on PowerPoint slides was usually too high, and that even fewer than 40 words on a slide resulted in "PowerPoint overload" that impeded understanding during presentations.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Mayer suggested a few major changes from traditional PowerPoint formats:<sup id="cite_ref-Mayer-Atkinson-2004_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayer-Atkinson-2004-16">[16]</a></sup></p>\n<ul>\n<li>replacing brief slide titles with longer "headlines" expressing complete ideas;</li>\n<li>showing more slides but simpler ones;</li>\n<li>removing almost all text including nearly all bullet lists (reserving the text for the spoken narration);</li>\n<li>using larger, higher-quality, and more important graphics and photographs;</li>\n<li>removing all extraneous decoration, backgrounds, logos and identifications, everything but the essential message.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Mayer\'s ideas are claimed by <a href="/wiki/Carmine_Gallo" title="Carmine Gallo">Carmine Gallo</a> to have been reflected in Steve Jobs\'s presentations: "Mayer outlined fundamental principles of multimedia design based on what scientists know about cognitive functioning. Steve Jobs\'s slides adhere to each of Mayer\'s principles ... ."<sup id="cite_ref-Gallo-2009_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallo-2009-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p92)</sup> Though not unique to Jobs, many people saw the style for the first time in Jobs\'s famous product introductions.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Steve Jobs would have been using Apple\'s <a href="/wiki/Keynote_(presentation_software)" title="Keynote (presentation software)">Keynote</a> which was designed for Jobs\'s own slide shows beginning in 2003, but Gallo says that "speaking like Jobs has little to do with the type of presentation software you use (PowerPoint, Keynote,etc.) ... all the techniques apply equally to PowerPoint and Keynote."<sup id="cite_ref-Gallo-2009_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallo-2009-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(pp14,46)</sup> Gallo adds that "Microsoft\'s PowerPoint has one big advantage over Apple\'s Keynote presentation software\xe2\x80\x94it\'s everywhere ... it\'s safe to say that the number of Keynote presentations is miniscule in comparison with PowerPoint. Although most presentation designers who are familiar with both formats prefer to work in the more elegant Keynote system, those same designers will tell you that the majority of their client work is done in PowerPoint."<sup id="cite_ref-Gallo-2009_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallo-2009-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(p44)</sup></p>\n<p>Consistent with its association with Steve Jobs\'s keynotes, a response to this style has been that it is particularly effective for "ballroom-style presentations" (as often given in conference center ballrooms) where a celebrated and practiced speaker addresses a large passive audience, but less appropriate for "conference room-style presentations" which are often recurring internal business meetings for in-depth discussion with motivated counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_it_better">Use it better</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Use it better">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Kosslyn" title="Stephen Kosslyn">Stephen Kosslyn</a></div>\n<p>A third reaction to PowerPoint use was to conclude that the standard style is capable of being used well, but that many small points need to be executed carefully, to avoid impeding understanding. This kind of analysis is particularly associated with <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Kosslyn" title="Stephen Kosslyn">Stephen Kosslyn</a>, a cognitive neuroscientist who specializes in the psychology of learning and visual communication, and who has been head of the department of psychology at Harvard, has been Director of Stanford\'s Center for Advanced Study in the Behavioral Sciences, and has published some 300 papers and 14 books.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Kosslyn presented a set of psychological principles of "human perception, memory, and comprehension" that "appears to capture the major points of agreement among researchers."<sup id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012-144">[144]</a></sup> He reports that his experiments support the idea that that it is not intuitive or obvious how to create effective PowerPoint presentations that conform to those agreed principles, and that even small differences that might not seem significant to a presenter can produce very different results in audiences\' understanding. For this reason, Kosslyn says, users need specific education to be able to identify best ways to avoid "flaws and failures":<sup id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012-144">[144]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>Specifically, we hypothesized and found that the psychological principles are often violated in PowerPoint slideshows across different fields ... , that some types of presentation flaws are noticeable and annoying to audience members ... , and that observers have difficulty identifying many violations in graphical displays in individual slides ... . These studies converge in painting the following picture: PowerPoint presentations are commonly flawed; some types of flaws are more common than others; flaws are not isolated to one domain or context; and, although some types of flaws annoy the audience, flaws at the level of slide design are not always obvious to an untrained observer ... .</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The many "flaws and failures" identified were those "likely to disrupt the comprehension or memory of the material." Among the most common examples were "Bulleted items are not presented individually, growing the list from the top to the bottom," "More than four bulleted items appear in a single list," "More than two lines are used per bulleted sentence," and "Words are not large enough (i.e., greater than 20 point) to be easily seen." Among audience reactions common problems reported were "Speakers read word-for-word from notes or from the slides themselves," "The slides contained too much material to absorb before the next slide was presented," and "The main point was obscured by lots of irrelevant detail."<sup id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012-144">[144]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Kosslyn observes that these findings could help to explain why the many studies of instructional effectiveness of PowerPoint have been inconclusive and conflicting, if there were differences in the quality of the presentations tested in different studies that went unobserved because "many may feel that \'good design\' is intuitively clear."<sup id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012_144-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012-144">[144]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2007 Kosslyn wrote a book about PowerPoint, in which he suggested a very large number of fairly modest changes to PowerPoint styles and gave advice on recommended ways of using PowerPoint.<sup id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-2007_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-2007-130">[130]</a></sup> In a later second book about PowerPoint he suggested nearly 150 clarifying style changes (in fewer than 150 pages).<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Kosslyn summarizes:<sup id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-2007_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-2007-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(pp2\xe2\x80\x933,200)</sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>... there\'s nothing fundamentally wrong with the PowerPoint program as a medium; rather, I claim that the problem lies in how it is used. ... In fact, this medium is a remarkably versatile tool that can be extraordinarily effective. ... For many purposes, PowerPoint presentations are a superior medium of communication, which is why they have become standard in so many fields.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>In 2017, an online poll of social media users in the UK was reported to show that PowerPoint "remains as popular with young tech-savvy users as it is with the Baby Boomers," with about four out of five saying that "PowerPoint was a great tool for making presentations," in part because "PowerPoint, with its capacity to be highly visual, bridges the wordy world of yesterday with the visual future of tomorrow."<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Also in 2017, the Managerial Communication Group of <a href="/wiki/MIT_Sloan_School_of_Management" title="MIT Sloan School of Management">MIT Sloan School of Management</a> polled their incoming MBA students, finding that "results underscore just how differently this generation communicates as compared with older workers."<sup id="cite_ref-mit-sloan-2017_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mit-sloan-2017-147">[147]</a></sup> Fewer than half of respondents reported doing any meaningful, longer-form writing at work, and even that minority mostly did so very infrequently, but "85 percent of students named producing presentations as a meaningful part of their job responsibilities. Two-thirds report that they present on a daily or weekly basis\xe2\x80\x94so it\'s no surprise that in-person presentations is the top skill they hope to improve."<sup id="cite_ref-mit-sloan-2017_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mit-sloan-2017-147">[147]</a></sup> One of the researchers concluded: "We\'re not likely to see future workplaces with long-form writing. The trend is toward presentations and slides, and we don\'t see any sign of that slowing down."<sup id="cite_ref-mit-sloan-2017_147-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mit-sloan-2017-147">[147]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._military_excess">U.S. military excess</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: U.S. military excess">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Use of PowerPoint by the U.S. military services began slowly, because they were invested in mainframe computers, MS-DOS PCs, and specialized military-specification graphic output devices, all of which PowerPoint did not support.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> But because of the strong military tradition of presenting <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/briefing" class="extiw" title="wikt:briefing">briefings</a>, as soon as they acquired the computers needed to run it, PowerPoint became part of the U.S. military.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>\n<p>By 2000, ten years after PowerPoint for Windows appeared, it was already identified as an important feature of U.S. armed forces culture, in a front-page story in the <i>Wall Street Journal</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ-Jaffe-2000_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-Jaffe-2000-150">[150]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>Old-fashioned slide briefings, designed to update generals on troop movements, have been a staple of the military since World War II. But in only a few short years PowerPoint has altered the landscape. Just as word processing made it easier to produce long, meandering memos, the spread of PowerPoint has unleashed a blizzard of jazzy but often incoherent visuals. Instead of drawing up a dozen slides on a legal pad and running them over to the graphics department, captains and colonels now can create hundreds of slides in a few hours without ever leaving their desks. If the spirit moves them they can build in gunfire sound effects and images that explode like land mines. ... PowerPoint has become such an ingrained part of the defense culture that it has seeped into the military lexicon. "PowerPoint Ranger" is a derogatory term for a desk-bound bureaucrat more adept at making slides than tossing grenades.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>U.S. military use of PowerPoint may have influenced its use by armed forces of other countries: "Foreign armed services also are beginning to get in on the act. \'You can\'t speak with the U.S. military without knowing PowerPoint,\' says Margaret Hayes, an instructor at National Defense University in Washington D.C., who teaches Latin American military officers how to use the software."<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ-Jaffe-2000_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-Jaffe-2000-150">[150]</a></sup></p>\n<p>After another 10 years, in 2010 (and again on its front page) the <i>New York Times</i> reported that PowerPoint use in the military was then "a military tool that has spun out of control":<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-Bumiller-2010_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-Bumiller-2010-151">[151]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>Like an insurgency, PowerPoint has crept into the daily lives of military commanders and reached the level of near obsession. The amount of time expended on PowerPoint, the Microsoft presentation program of computer-generated charts, graphs and bullet points, has made it a running joke in the Pentagon and in Iraq and Afghanistan. ... Commanders say that behind all the PowerPoint jokes are serious concerns that the program stifles discussion, critical thinking and thoughtful decision-making. Not least, it ties up junior officers ... in the daily preparation of slides, be it for a Joint Staff meeting in Washington or for a platoon leader\'s pre-mission combat briefing in a remote pocket of Afghanistan.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The <i>New York Times</i> account went on to say that as a result some U.S. generals had banned the use of PowerPoint in their operations:<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-Bumiller-2010_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-Bumiller-2010-151">[151]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>"PowerPoint makes us stupid," Gen. <a href="/wiki/James_Mattis" title="James Mattis">James N. Mattis</a> of the Marine Corps, the Joint Forces commander, said this month at a military conference in North Carolina. (He spoke without PowerPoint.) Brig. Gen. <a href="/wiki/H._R._McMaster" title="H. R. McMaster">H. R. McMaster</a>, who banned PowerPoint presentations when he led the successful effort to secure the northern Iraqi city of Tal Afar in 2005, followed up at the same conference by likening PowerPoint to an internal threat. "It\'s dangerous because it can create the illusion of understanding and the illusion of control," General McMaster said in a telephone interview afterward. "Some problems in the world are not bullet-izable."</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Several incidents, about the same time, gave wide currency to discussions by serving military officers describing excessive PowerPoint use and the organizational culture that encouraged it.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> In response to the <i>New York Times</i> story, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Norvig" title="Peter Norvig">Peter Norvig</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Kosslyn" title="Stephen Kosslyn">Stephen M. Kosslyn</a> sent a joint letter to the editor stressing the institutional culture of the military: "... many military personnel bemoan the overuse and misuse of PowerPoint. ... The problem is not in the tool itself, but in the way that people use it\xe2\x80\x94which is partly a result of how institutions promote misuse.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The two generals who had been mentioned in 2010 as opposing the institutional culture of excessive PowerPoint use were both in the news again in 2017, when <a href="/wiki/James_Mattis" title="James Mattis">James N. Mattis</a> became U.S. Secretary of Defense,<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/H._R._McMaster" title="H. R. McMaster">H. R.
