McMaster</a> was appointed as U.S. National Security Advisor.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistic_medium">Artistic medium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Artistic medium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Musician <a href="/wiki/David_Byrne" title="David Byrne">David Byrne</a> has been using PowerPoint as a medium for art for years, producing a book and DVD and showing at galleries his PowerPoint-based artwork.<sup id="cite_ref-Vienne_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vienne-120">[120]</a></sup> Byrne has written: "I have been working with PowerPoint, the ubiquitous presentation software, as an art medium for a number of years. It started off as a joke (this software is a symbol of corporate salesmanship, or lack thereof) but then the work took on a life of its own as I realized I could create pieces that were moving, despite the limitations of the \'medium.<span style="padding-right:.2em;">\'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2005 Byrne toured with a theater piece styled as a PowerPoint presentation. When he presented it in Berkeley, on March 8, 2005, the University of California news service reported: "Byrne also defended its [PowerPoint\'s] appeal as more than just a business tool\xe2\x80\x94as a medium for art and theater. His talk was titled \'I \xe2\x99\xa5 PowerPoint\' ... . Berkeley alumnus Bob Gaskins and Dennis Austin ... were in the audience ... . Eventually, Byrne said, PowerPoint could be the foundation for \'presentational theater,\' with roots in Brechtian drama and Asian puppet theater."<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> After that performance, Byrne described it in his own online journal: "Did the PowerPoint talk in Berkeley for an audience of IT legends and academics. I was terrified. The guys that originally turned PowerPoint into a program were there, what were THEY gonna think? ... [Gaskins] did tell me afterwards that he liked the PowerPoint as theater idea, which was a relief."<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The expressions "PowerPoint Art" or "<a href="/wiki/PptArt" title="PptArt">pptArt</a>" are used to define a contemporary Italian artistic movement which believes that the corporate world can be a unique and exceptional source of inspiration for the artist.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> They say: "The pptArt name refers to PowerPoint, the symbolic and abstract language developed by the corporate world which has become a universal and highly symbolic communication system beyond cultures and borders."<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The wide use of PowerPoint had, by 2010, given rise to " ... a subculture of PowerPoint enthusiasts [that] is teaching the old application new tricks, and may even be turning a dry presentation format into a full-fledged artistic medium,"<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> by using <a href="/wiki/PowerPoint_animation" title="PowerPoint animation">PowerPoint animation</a> to create "games, artworks, anime, and movies."<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="PowerPoint_Viewer">PowerPoint Viewer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: PowerPoint Viewer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>PowerPoint Viewer is the name for a series of small free application programs to be used on computers without PowerPoint installed, to view, project, or print (but not create or edit) presentations.<sup id="cite_ref-view-presentation-without-ppt_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-view-presentation-without-ppt-166">[166]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The first version was introduced with PowerPoint 3.0 in 1992, to enable electronic presentations to be projected using conference-room computers and to be freely distributed; on Windows, it took advantage of the new feature of embedding <a href="/wiki/TrueType" title="TrueType">TrueType</a> fonts within PowerPoint presentation files to make such distribution easier.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The same kind of viewer app was shipped with PowerPoint 3.0 for Macintosh, also in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Beginning with PowerPoint 2003, a feature called "Package for CD" automatically managed all linked video and audio files plus needed fonts when exporting a presentation to a disk or flash drive or network location,<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> and also included a copy of a revised PowerPoint Viewer application so that the result could be presented on other PCs without installing anything.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The latest version that runs on Windows "was created in conjunction with PowerPoint 2010, but it can also be used to view newer presentations created in PowerPoint 2013 and PowerPoint 2016. ... All transitions, videos and effects appear and behave the same when viewed using PowerPoint Viewer as they do when viewed in PowerPoint 2010." It supports presentations created using PowerPoint 97 and later.<sup id="cite_ref-view-presentation-without-ppt_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-view-presentation-without-ppt-166">[166]</a></sup> The latest version that runs on Macintosh is PowerPoint 98 Viewer for the <a href="/wiki/Classic_Mac_OS" title="Classic Mac OS">Classic Mac OS</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_macOS_components#Classic" title="List of macOS components">Classic Environment</a>, for Macs supporting System 7.5 to <a href="/wiki/Mac_OS_X_Tiger" title="Mac OS X Tiger">Mac OS X Tiger</a> (10.4).<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> It can open presentations only from PowerPoint 3.0, 4.0, and 8.0 (PowerPoint 98), although presentations created on Mac can be opened in PowerPoint Viewer on Windows.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the latest versions of PowerPoint Viewer for Windows (2010)<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> and for Macintosh (1998)<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> remain available for download.</p>\n<p>Since PowerPoint 2013, the recommended replacement for PowerPoint Viewer has been to use any computer with a browser and network access to display any accessible PowerPoint presentation by using the free web app <a href="/wiki/Office_Online" title="Office Online">PowerPoint Online</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Versions">Versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span style="display:none;speak:none">000000002017-06-01-0000</span><span style="white-space:nowrap">June 2017</span><sup id="cite_ref-ppt-16-winstore-jun-2017_224-0" class="reference"><a editions">Windows 10 S</a></td>\n<td></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Date</th>\n<th>Name</th>\n<th>Version</th>\n<th>System</th>\n<th>Comments</th>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div on Windows 7</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:66px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Powerpoint_mac_2008_icon.png" class="image"><img alt="" class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Powerpoint_mac_2008_icon.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nIcon for PowerPoint for Mac 2008</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png/220px-Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d6/Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png 2x" data-file-width="387" data-file-height="258" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMicrosoft PowerPoint for Mac 2011</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 1.0</dt>\n<dd>For Macintosh: April 1987<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-1-mac-apr-1987_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-1-mac-apr-1987-176">[176]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Innovations included: multiple slides in a single file, organizing slides with a slide sorter view and a title view (precursor of outline view), speakers\' notes pages attached to each slide, printing of audience handouts with multiple slides per page, text with outlining styles and full word-processor formatting, graphic shapes with attached text for drawing diagrams and tables.<sup id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-225">[225]</a></sup> It also shipped with a hardbound book as its manual.<sup id="cite_ref-belleville-ppthistory-2000_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belleville-ppthistory-2000-226">[226]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>"It produced overhead transparencies on a black-and-white Macintosh for laser printing. Presenters could now directly control their own overheads and would no longer have to work through the person with the typewriter. PowerPoint handled the task of making the overheads all look alike; one change reformats them all. Typographic fonts were better than an Orator typeball, and charts and diagrams could be imported from MacDraw, MacPaint, and Excel, thanks to the new Mac clipboard."<sup id="cite_ref-gaskins-cacm-2007_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gaskins-cacm-2007-227">[227]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Mac) Original Macintosh or better, System 1.0 or higher, 512K RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">[228]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 2.0</dt>\n<dd>For Macintosh: May 1988;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-2-mac-may-1988_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-2-mac-may-1988-178">[178]</a></sup> for Windows: May 1990<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-2-win-may-1990_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-2-win-may-1990-180">[180]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_for_Mac" class="mw-redirect" title="Microsoft Office for Mac">Microsoft Office for Mac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office#Windows_versions" title="Microsoft Office">Microsoft Office for Windows</a>. Innovations included: color, more word processing features, find and replace, spell checking, color schemes for presentations, guide to color selection, ability to change color scheme retrospectively, shaded coloring for fills.<sup id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001_225-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-225">[225]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>"It added color 35mm slides, transmitting the resulting file over a modem to Genigraphics for imaging on Genigraphics\' film recorders and photo processing in Genigraphics\' labs overnight. Genigraphics was the leading professional service bureau, having developed its own Digital Equipment Corp. PDP-11-based computer systems for its artists. After a short time, though, Genigraphics itself switched to PowerPoint."<sup id="cite_ref-gaskins-cacm-2007_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gaskins-cacm-2007-227">[227]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Mac) Original Macintosh or better, System 4.1 or higher, 1 MB RAM. (Windows) 286 PC or higher, Windows 3.0, 1 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">[228]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 3.0</dt>\n<dd>For Windows, May 1992;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-3-win-may-1992_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-3-win-may-1992-181">[181]</a></sup> for Mac: September 1992<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-3-mac-sep-1992_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-3-mac-sep-1992-182">[182]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_3.0" title="Microsoft Office 3.0">Microsoft Office for Windows 3.0</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office#Mac_versions" title="Microsoft Office">Microsoft Office for Mac 3.0</a>. Innovations included: the first application designed exclusively for the new Windows 3.1 platform, full support for TrueType fonts (new in Windows 3.1), presentation templates, editing in outline view, new drawing, including freeform tool, autoshapes, flip, rotate, scale, align, and transforming imported pictures into their drawing primitives to make them editable, transitions between slides in slide show, progressive builds, incorporating sound and video.<sup id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001_225-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-225">[225]</a></sup> Animations included "flying bullets" where bullet points "flew" into the slide one by one, and some degree of Pen Computing support was included.<sup id="cite_ref-belleville-ppthistory-2000_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belleville-ppthistory-2000-226">[226]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>"It added video-out to feed the new video projectors, with effects that could replace a bank of synchronized slide projectors. This version added fades, dissolves, and other transitions, as well as animation of text and pictures, and could incorporate video clips with synchronized audio."<sup id="cite_ref-gaskins-cacm-2007_227-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gaskins-cacm-2007-227">[227]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) 286 PC or higher, Windows 3.1, 2 MB RAM. (Mac) Macintosh Plus or better, System 7 or higher, 4 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">[228]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 4.0</dt>\n<dd>For Windows: February 1994;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-4-win-feb-1994_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-4-win-feb-1994-183">[183]</a></sup> for Mac: October 1994<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-4-mac-oct-1994_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-4-mac-oct-1994-184">[184]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office#Windows_versions" title="Microsoft Office">Microsoft Office for Windows 4.0</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office#Mac_versions" title="Microsoft Office">Microsoft Office for Mac 4.2</a>. Innovations included: autolayouts, Word tables, rehearsal mode, hidden slides, and the "AutoContent Wizard."<sup id="cite_ref-belleville-ppthistory-2000_226-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belleville-ppthistory-2000-226">[226]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Introduced a standard "Microsoft Office" look and feel (shared with Word and Excel), with status bar, toolbars, tooltips. Full <a href="/wiki/Object_Linking_and_Embedding" title="Object Linking and Embedding">OLE</a> 2.0 with in-place activation.<sup id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001_225-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-225">[225]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) 386 PC or higher, Windows 3.1, 8 MB RAM. (Mac) 68020 Mac or better, System 7 or higher, 8 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">[228]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 7.0</dt>\n<dd>For Windows: July 1995<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-7-win-jul-1995_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-7-win-jul-1995-185">[185]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_95" title="Microsoft Office 95">Microsoft Office for Windows 95</a>. Innovations included: new animation effects, real curves and textures, black and white view, autocorrect, insert symbol, meeting support features such as "Meeting Minder."<sup id="cite_ref-belleville-ppthistory-2000_226-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belleville-ppthistory-2000-226">[226]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>"A complete rewrite of the product from the ground up in C++, full object model with internal <a href="/wiki/Visual_Basic_for_Applications" title="Visual Basic for Applications">VBA</a> programmability."<sup id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001_225-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-225">[225]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) 386 DX PC or higher, Windows 95, 6 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">[228]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 8.0</dt>\n<dd>For Windows: January 1997;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-8-win-jan-1997_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-8-win-jan-1997-187">[187]</a></sup> for Mac: March 1998<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-8-mac-mar-1998_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-8-mac-mar-1998-188">[188]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_97" title="Microsoft Office 97">Microsoft Office for Windows 97</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_98_Macintosh_Edition" title="Microsoft Office 98 Macintosh Edition">Microsoft Office 98 Macintosh Edition</a>. Innovations included: "Office Assistant," file compression, save to HTML, "Pack and Go," "AutoClipArt," transparent GIFs.<sup id="cite_ref-belleville-ppthistory-2000_226-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belleville-ppthistory-2000-226">[226]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) 486 PC or higher, 8 MB RAM. (Mac) PowerPC Mac or better, 16 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">[228]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 9.0</dt>\n<dd>For Windows: June 1999;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-9-win-jun-1999_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-9-win-jun-1999-190">[190]</a></sup> for Mac: August 2000<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-9-mac-aug-2000_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-9-mac-aug-2000-191">[191]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_2000" title="Microsoft Office 2000">Microsoft Office for Windows 2000</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office#Mac_versions" title="Microsoft Office">Microsoft Office for Mac 2001</a>. Innovations included: three-pane "browser" view (selectable list of slide miniatures or titles, large single slide, notes), autofit text, real tables, presentation conferencing, save to web, picture bullets, animated GIFs, aliased fonts.<sup id="cite_ref-belleville-ppthistory-2000_226-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belleville-ppthistory-2000-226">[226]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) Pentium 75MHz+, Windows 95 or higher, 20 MB RAM. (Mac) PowerPC Mac 120MHz+ or better, MacOS 8.5 or higher, minimum 48 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">[228]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 10.0</dt>\n<dd>For Windows: May 2001;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-10-win-may-2001_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-10-win-may-2001-192">[192]</a></sup> for Mac: November 2001<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-10-mac-nov-2001_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-10-mac-nov-2001-193">[193]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_XP" title="Microsoft Office XP">Microsoft Office for Windows XP</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office#Mac_versions" title="Microsoft Office">Microsoft Office for Mac v.X</a>. Innovations included: install from web, most clipart on web, use of Exchange and SharePoint for storage and collaboration.<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-10-win-may-2001_192-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-10-win-may-2001-192">[192]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) Pentium III, Windows 98 or higher, 40 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">[228]</a></sup> (Mac) OS X 10.1 ("Puma") or later (will not run under OS 9).<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-10-mac-nov-2001-os-x-version_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-10-mac-nov-2001-os-x-version-229">[229]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 11.0</dt>\n<dd>For Windows: October 2003;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-11-win-oct-2003_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-11-win-oct-2003-194">[194]</a></sup> for Mac: June 2004;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-11-mac-jun-2004_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-11-mac-jun-2004-196">[196]</a></sup> for Mobile: May 2005<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-11-mob-may-2005_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-11-mob-may-2005-197">[197]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_2003" title="Microsoft Office 2003">Microsoft Office for Windows 2003</a> and <a href="/wiki/Office_2004_for_Mac" title="Office 2004 for Mac">Microsoft Office for Mac 2004</a>. Innovations included: tools visible to presenter during slide show (notes, thumbnails, time clock, re-order and edit slides), "Package for CD" to write presentation and viewer app to CD.<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-11-mac-jun-2004_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-11-mac-jun-2004-196">[196]</a></sup> "Microsoft Producer for PowerPoint 2003" was a free plug-in from Microsoft, using a video camera, "that creates Web page presentations, with talking head narration, coordinated and timed to your existing PowerPoint presentation" for delivery over the web.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> The Genigraphics software to send a presentation for imaging as 35mm slides was removed from this version.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) Pentium 233Mhz+, Windows XP or later, 128 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> (Mac) Power Mac G3 or better, OS X 10.2.8 or later, 256 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-11-mac-jun-2004_196-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-11-mac-jun-2004-196">[196]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 12.0</dt>\n<dd>For Windows: January 2007;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-12-win-jan-2007_198-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-12-win-jan-2007-198">[198]</a></sup> for Mobile: September 2007;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-12-mob-sep-2007_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-12-mob-sep-2007-200">[200]</a></sup> for Mac: January 2008<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-12-mac-jan-2008_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-12-mac-jan-2008-201">[201]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_2007" title="Microsoft Office 2007">Microsoft Office for Windows 2007</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_2008_for_Mac" title="Microsoft Office 2008 for Mac">Microsoft Office for Mac 2008</a>. Innovations included: new user interface ("Office Fluent") employing a changeable "ribbon" of tools across the top to replace menus and toolbars, SmartArt graphics, many graphical improvements in text and drawing, improved "Presenter View" (from 2003), widescreen slide formats. The "AutoContent Wizard" was removed from this version.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>A major change in PowerPoint 2007 was from a binary file format, used from 1997 to 2003, to a new XML file format which evolved over further versions.</dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) 500 MHz processor or higher, Windows XP with SP2 or later, 256 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> (Mac) 500 MHz processor or higher, MacOS X 10.4.9 or later, 512 MB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 14.0<sup id="cite_ref-why-no-13_203-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-why-no-13-203">[203]</a></sup></dt>\n<dd>For Windows: June 2010;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-14-win-jun-2010_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-14-win-jun-2010-202">[202]</a></sup> for Web: June 2010;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-14-web-jun-2010_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-14-web-jun-2010-204">[204]</a></sup> for Mobile: June 2010;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-14-mob-jun-2010_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-14-mob-jun-2010-205">[205]</a></sup> for Mac: November 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-14-mac-nov-2010_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-14-mac-nov-2010-206">[206]</a></sup> for Symbian: April 2012<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-14-symbian-apr-2012_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-14-symbian-apr-2012-208">[208]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_2010" title="Microsoft Office 2010">Microsoft Office for Windows 2010</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_for_Mac_2011" title="Microsoft Office for Mac 2011">Microsoft Office for Mac 2011</a>. Innovations included: Single document interface (SDI), sections within presentations, reading view, redesign of "Backstage" functions (under File menu), save as video, insert video from web, embed video and audio, enhanced editing for video and for pictures, broadcast slideshow.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) 500 MHz processor or higher, Windows XP with SP3 or later, 256 MB RAM, 512 MB RAM recommended for video.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> (Mac) Intel processor, Mac OS X 10.5.8 or later, 1 GB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 15.0</dt>\n<dd>For Web: October 2012;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-15-web-oct-2012_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-15-web-oct-2012-209">[209]</a></sup> for Mobile: November 2012;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-15-mob-nov-2012_210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-15-mob-nov-2012-210">[210]</a></sup> for Windows RT: November 2012;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-15-winrt-nov-2012_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-15-winrt-nov-2012-211">[211]</a></sup> for Windows: January 2013;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-15-win-jan-2013_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-15-win-jan-2013-212">[212]</a></sup> for iPhone: June 2013;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-15-iphone-jun-2013_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-15-iphone-jun-2013-213">[213]</a></sup> for Android: July 2013;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-15-android-jul-2013_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-15-android-jul-2013-214">[214]</a></sup> for Web: February 2014;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-15-online-feb-2014_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-15-online-feb-2014-215">[215]</a></sup> for iPad: March 2014;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-15-ipad-mar-2014_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-15-ipad-mar-2014-216">[216]</a></sup> for iOS: November 2014;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-15-ios-nov-2014_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-15-mac-jul-2015-219">[219]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_2013" title="Microsoft Office 2013">Microsoft Office for Windows 2013</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_2016#Mac" title="Microsoft Office 2016">Microsoft Office for Mac 2016</a>. Innovations included: Change default slide shape to 16:9 aspect ratio, online collaboration by multiple authors, user interface redesigned for multi-touch screens, improved audio, video, animations, and transitions, further changes to Presenter View. Clipart collections (and insertion tool) were removed, but available online.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">\xe2\x80\x89</span><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) 1 GHz processor or faster, x86- or x64-bit processor with SSE2 instruction set, Windows 7 or later, 1 GB RAM (32-bit), 2 GB RAM (64-bit).<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> (Mac) Intel processor, Mac OS X 10.10 or later, 4 GB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-2016-15-mac-sys-req_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-2016-15-mac-sys-req-242">[242]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<dl>\n<dt>PowerPoint 16.0</dt>\n<dd>For Android: June 2015;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-16-android-jun-2015_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-16-android-jun-2015-218">[218]</a></sup> for Mobile: July 2015;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-16-mob-jul-2015_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-16-mob-jul-2015-221">[221]</a></sup> for iOS: July 2015;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-16-ios-jul-2015_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-16-ios-jul-2015-222">[222]</a></sup> for Windows: September 2015;<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-16-win-sep-2015_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-16-win-sep-2015-223">[223]</a></sup> and Windows Store: June 2017<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-16-winstore-jun-2017_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-16-winstore-jun-2017-224">[224]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>Part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_2016#Windows" title="Microsoft Office 2016">Microsoft Office for Windows 2016</a>. Innovations included: "Tell me" to search for program controls, "PowerPoint Designer" pane, Morph transition, real-time collaboration, "Zoom" to slides or sections in slideshow,<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> and "Presentation Translator" for real-time translation of a presenter\'s spoken words to on-screen captions in any of 60+ languages, with the system analyzing the text of the PowerPoint presentation as context to increase the accuracy and relevance of the translations.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">\xe2\x80\x89</span><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup></dd>\n<dd>System requirements: (Windows) 1 GHz processor or faster, x86- or x64-bit processor with SSE2 instruction set, Windows 7 with SP 1 or later, 2 GB RAM.<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-2016-16-win-sys-req_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-2016-16-win-sys-req-246">[246]</a></sup></dd>\n</dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="File_formats">File formats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: File formats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em">\n<caption style="font-size:130%; padding-bottom:0.15em;">PowerPoint Presentation</caption>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/Filename_extension" title="Filename extension">Filename extensions</a></th>\n<td style="line-height:1.35em;"><code>.pptx, .ppt<sup id="cite_ref-file-formats-with-note_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-file-formats-with-note-247">[247]</a></sup></code></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/Media_type" title="Media type">Internet media&#160;type</a></th>\n<td style="line-height:1.35em;"><code>application/vnd.openxmlformats-officedocument.presentationml.presentation, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint<sup id="cite_ref-mime-types_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mime-types-248">[248]</a></sup></code></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/Uniform_Type_Identifier" title="Uniform Type Identifier">Uniform Type Identifier&#160;(UTI)</a></th>\n<td style="line-height:1.35em;">com.microsoft.powerpoint.ppt<sup id="cite_ref-type-codes_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-type-codes-249">[249]</a></sup></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">Developed&#160;by</th>\n<td style="line-height:1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">Type of format</th>\n<td style="line-height:1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/Presentation" title="Presentation">Presentation</a></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h3><span id="Binary_(1987\xe2\x80\x932007)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Binary_.281987.E2.80.932007.29">Binary (1987\xe2\x80\x932007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Binary (1987\xe2\x80\x932007)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Early versions of PowerPoint, from 1987 through 1995 (versions 1.0 through 7.0), evolved through a sequence of binary file formats, different in each version, as functionality was added.<sup id="cite_ref-early-file-compatibility_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-early-file-compatibility-250">[250]</a></sup> That resulted in a stable binary format (called a .ppt file, like all earlier binary formats) that was shared as the default in PowerPoint 97 through PowerPoint 2003 for Windows, and in PowerPoint 98 through PowerPoint 2004 for Mac (that is, in PowerPoint versions 8.0 through 11.0).<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-binary-doc_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-binary-doc-251">[251]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> The specification document is actively maintained and can be freely downloaded,<sup id="cite_ref-ppt-binary-doc_251-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ppt-binary-doc-251">[251]</a></sup> because, although no longer the default, that binary format can be read and written by some later versions of PowerPoint, including the current PowerPoint 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-file-formats-with-note_247-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-file-formats-with-note-247">[247]</a></sup> After the stable binary format was adopted, versions of PowerPoint continued to be able to read and write differing file formats from earlier versions.<sup id="cite_ref-early-file-compatibility_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-early-file-compatibility-250">[250]</a></sup> But beginning with PowerPoint 2007 and PowerPoint 2008 for Mac (PowerPoint version 12.0), this was the only binary format available for saving; PowerPoint 2007 (version 12.0) no longer supported saving to binary file formats used earlier than PowerPoint 97 (version 8.0), ten years before.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup></p>\n<p><b>Binary <a href="/wiki/Filename_extension" title="Filename extension">filename extensions</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-file-formats-with-note_247-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-file-formats-with-note-247">[247]</a></sup></p>\n<ul>\n<li>.ppt, PowerPoint 97\xe2\x80\x932003 binary presentation</li>\n<li>.pps, PowerPoint 97\xe2\x80\x932003 binary slide show</li>\n<li>.pot, PowerPoint 97\xe2\x80\x932003 binary template</li>\n</ul>\n<p><b>Binary <a href="/wiki/Media_type" title="Media type">media types</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-mime-types_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mime-types-248">[248]</a></sup></p>\n<ul>\n<li>.ppt, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint</li>\n<li>.pps, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint</li>\n<li>.pot, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint</li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span id="Office_Open_XML_(since_2007)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Office_Open_XML_.28since_2007.29">Office Open XML (since 2007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Office Open XML (since 2007)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The big change in PowerPoint 2007 and PowerPoint 2008 for Mac (PowerPoint version 12.0) was that the stable binary file format of 97\xe2\x80\x932003 was replaced as the default by a new <a href="/wiki/Zip_(file_format)" title="Zip (file format)">zipped</a> <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">XML</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Office_Open_XML" title="Office Open XML">Office Open XML</a> format (.pptx files).<sup id="cite_ref-open-xml-formats_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-open-xml-formats-254">[254]</a></sup> Microsoft\'s explanation of the benefits of the change included: smaller file sizes, up to 75% smaller than comparable binary documents; security, through being able to identify and exclude executable macros and personal data; less chance to be corrupted than binary formats; and easier interoperability for exchanging data among Microsoft and other business applications, all while maintaining backward compatibility.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup></p>\n<p><b>XML <a href="/wiki/Filename_extension" title="Filename extension">filename extensions</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-file-formats-with-note_247-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-file-formats-with-note-247">[247]</a></sup></p>\n<ul>\n<li>.pptx, PowerPoint 2007 XML presentation</li>\n<li>.pptm, PowerPoint 2007 XML macro-enabled presentation</li>\n<li>.ppsx, PowerPoint 2007 XML slide show</li>\n<li>.ppsm, PowerPoint 2007 XML macro-enabled slide show</li>\n<li>.ppam, PowerPoint 2007 XML add-in</li>\n<li>.potx, PowerPoint 2007 XML template</li>\n<li>.potm, PowerPoint 2007 XML macro-enabled template</li>\n</ul>\n<p><b>XML <a href="/wiki/Media_type" title="Media type">media types</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-mime-types_248-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mime-types-248">[248]</a></sup></p>\n<ul>\n<li>.pptx, application/vnd.openxmlformats-officedocument.presentationml.presentation</li>\n<li>.pptm, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint.presentation.macroEnabled.12</li>\n<li>.ppsx, application/vnd.openxmlformats-officedocument.presentationml.slideshow</li>\n<li>.ppsm, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint.slideshow.macroEnabled.12</li>\n<li>.ppam, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint.addin.macroEnabled.12</li>\n<li>.potx, application/vnd.openxmlformats-officedocument.presentationml.template</li>\n<li>.potm, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint.template.macroEnabled.12</li>\n</ul>\n<p>The specification for the new format was published as an <a href="/wiki/Open_standard" title="Open standard">open standard</a>, <a href="/wiki/ECMA-376" class="mw-redirect" title="ECMA-376">ECMA-376</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> through <a href="/wiki/Ecma_International" title="Ecma International">Ecma International</a> Technical Committee 45 (TC45).<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> The Ecma 376 stardard was approved in December 2006, and was submitted for standardization through <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_JTC_1/SC_34" title="ISO/IEC JTC 1/SC 34">ISO/IEC JTC 1/SC 34</a> WG4 in early 2007. The <a href="/wiki/Standardization_of_Office_Open_XML" title="Standardization of Office Open XML">standardization process</a> was contentious.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> It was approved as <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_29500" class="mw-redirect" title="ISO/IEC 29500">ISO/IEC 29500</a> in early 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup> Copies of the ISO/IEC standard specification are freely available, in two parts.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> These define two related standards known as "Transitional" and "Strict." The two standards were progressively adopted by PowerPoint: PowerPoint version 12.0 (2007, 2008 for Mac) could read and write Transitional format, but could neither read nor write Strict format. PowerPoint version 14.0 (2010, 2011 for Mac) could read and write Transitional, and also read but not write Strict. PowerPoint version 15.0 and later (beginning 2013, 2016 for Mac) can read and write both Transitional and Strict formats. The reason for the two variants was explained by Microsoft:<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup></p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>... the participants in the ISO/IEC standardization process recognized two objectives with competing requirements. The first objective was for the Open XML standard to provide an XML-based file format that could fully support conversion of the billions of existing Office documents without any loss of features, content, text, layout, or other information, including embedded data. The second was to specify a file format that did not rely on Microsoft-specific data types. They created two variants of Open XML\xe2\x80\x94Transitional, which supports previously-defined Microsoft-specific data types, and Strict, which does not rely on them. Prior versions of Office [that is, 2007] have supported reading and writing Transitional Open XML, and Office 2010 can read Strict Open XML documents. With the addition of write support for Strict Open XML, Office 2013 provides full support for both variants of Open XML.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The PowerPoint .pptx file format (called "<a href="/wiki/PresentationML" class="mw-redirect" title="PresentationML">PresentationML</a>" for Presentation Markup Language) contains separate structures for all the complex parts of a PowerPoint presentation.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> The specification documents run to over six&#160;thousand pages.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> Because of the widespread use of PowerPoint, the standardized file formats are considered important for the long-term access to digital documents in library collections and archives, according to the U.S. Library of Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup></p>\n<p>PowerPoint 2013 and PowerPoint 2016 provide options to set default saving to ISO/IEC 29500 Strict format, but the initial default setting remains Transitional, for compatibility with legacy features incorporating binary data in existing documents.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> PowerPoint 2013 or PowerPoint 2016 will both open and save files in the former binary format (.ppt), for compatibility with older versions of the program (but not versions older than PowerPoint 97).<sup id="cite_ref-file-formats-with-note_247-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-file-formats-with-note-247">[247]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup> In saving to older formats, these versions of PowerPoint will check to assure that no features have been introduced into the presentation which are incompatible with the older formats.<sup id="cite_ref-open-xml-formats_254-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-open-xml-formats-254">[254]</a></sup></p>\n<p>PowerPoint 2013 and 2016 will also save a presentation in many other file formats, including <a href="/wiki/Portable_Document_Format" title="Portable Document Format">PDF</a> format, <a href="/wiki/MPEG-4" title="MPEG-4">MPEG-4</a> or <a href="/wiki/Windows_Media_Video" title="Windows Media Video">WMV</a> video, as a sequence of single-picture files (using image formats including <a href="/wiki/GIF" title="GIF">GIF</a>, <a href="/wiki/JPEG" title="JPEG">JPEG</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portable_Network_Graphics" title="Portable Network Graphics">PNG</a>, <a href="/wiki/TIFF" title="TIFF">TIFF</a>, and some older formats), and as a single presentation file in which all slides are replaced with pictures. PowerPoint will both open and save files in <a href="/wiki/OpenDocument_technical_specification#Document_representation" title="OpenDocument technical specification">OpenDocument Presentation</a> format (ODP) for compatibility.<sup id="cite_ref-file-formats-with-note_247-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-file-formats-with-note-247">[247]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<dl>\n<dt>Similar apps</dt>\n</dl>\n<ul>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Google_Slides" class="mw-redirect" title="Google Slides">Google Slides</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Keynote_(presentation_software)" title="Keynote (presentation software)">Keynote (presentation software)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/LibreOffice_Impress" class="mw-redirect" title="LibreOffice Impress">LibreOffice Impress</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Apache_OpenOffice" title="Apache OpenOffice">OpenOffice Impress</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Calligra_Stage" title="Calligra Stage">Calligra Stage</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Prezi" title="Prezi">Prezi</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/AppleWorks" The special edition, called The Microsoft Office, includes Word 4.0, Excel 2.2, PowerPoint 2.01, and Mail 1.37. The package sells for $849; if purchased separately, the programs would cost $1,310, the company said. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">September 23,</span> 2017</span>. <q>Because every day a huge number of people meet to exchange ideas and make decisions with PowerPoint slides being displayed on the wall, investigating the tool is enormously important ... . Despite the pervasiveness of PowerPoint in our culture there have been few empirical studies and most of the non-empirical work is based on casual essays and informal anecdotal reviews which very often take a polemic and overall negative position on PowerPoint, rather than conducting formal scholarship. This lack of rigorous studies and empirical research is surprising given the enormous complexity and importance of the PowerPoint tool.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMicrosoft+PowerPoint&amp;rft.au=Bresciani%2C+Sabrina&amp;rft.aufirst=Sebastian&amp;rft.aulast=Kernbach&amp;rft.btitle=10+Years+after+Tufte%27s+%22Cognitive+Style+of+PowerPoint%22%3A+Synthesizing+its+Constraining+Qualities&amp;rft.date=2013-07-16%2F2013-07-18&amp;rft.genre=conference&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4799-0834-9&amp;rft.pages=345-350&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.pub=IEEE&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fieeexplore.ieee.org%2Fstamp%2Fstamp.jsp%3Farnumber%3D6676584&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1109%2FIV.2013.44&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.psych.ucsb.edu/people/faculty/mayer">"Richard Mayer"</a>. <i>Psychological and Brain Sciences department, University of California at Santa Barbara, faculty directory</i>. 2017. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170617030504/https://www.psych.ucsb.edu/people/faculty/mayer">Archived</a> from the original on June 17, 2017<span class="reference-accessdate">.
